صاحب قدوری فرماتے ہیں کہ مذکورہ دونوں صورتوں میں یعنی جاریہ اور طوق کی صورت میں اور تلوار اور حلیہ کی صورت میں اگر مجلس میں باہم قبضہ نہ ہوا اور عاقدین دونوں جدا ہو گئے تو طوق اور حلیہ کے حصہ میں عقد باطل ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ حصہ طوق اور حصہ حلیہ میں دونوں عوضوں کے ثمن یعنی چاندی ہونے کی وجہ سے یہ عقد صرف ہے اور بغیر قبضہ کئے جدا ہونے سے چونکہ عقد صرف باطل ہو جاتا ہے۔ اسلئے حصہ طوق اور حصہ حلیہ میں یہ عقد باطل ہو جائے گا۔ اور باندی کے اندر درست ہو جائے گا۔ اور رہا تلوار کا معاملہ تو حلیہ اگر تلوار کے ساتھ ایسے طریقہ سے پیوستہ اور وابستہ ہے کہ حلیہ کو تلوار سے جدا کرنا بغیر ضرر کے ممکن نہ ہو تو تلوار کی بیع بھی باطل ہو جائے گی ۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں بغیر ضرر کے تلوار کا سپر د کر ناممکن نہ رہا اور جس صورت میں بغیر ضرر کے مبیع سپر ذکر ناممکن نہ ہو ۔ اس صورت میں بیچ باطل ہو جاتی ہے اسلئے مذکورہ صورت میں تلوار کی بیع بھی باطل ہو جائے گی ۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ جب حلیہ کا تلوار سے جدا کرنا بغیر ضرر کے ممکن نہ ہو تو تنہا تلوار کی بیع جائز نہیں ہوتی جیسے چھت میں لگے ہوئے شہتیر کا سپر د کرنا چونکہ بغیر ضرر کے ممکن نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے چھت میں لگے ہوئے شہتیر کا بیچانا جائز ہے اور اگر حلیہ کا تلوار سے جدا کرنا بغیر ضرر کے ممکن ہو تو تلوار کی بیع جائز ہو جائے گی اور حلیہ کی بیع باطل ہو جائے گی ۔ کہونا اس صورت میں تلوار یعنی بیج کو بغیر ضرر کے سپرد کرناممکن ہے اور جس صورت میں مبیع کو بغیر ضرر کے سپرد کرناممکن ہو اس صورت میں بیع جائز ہوتی ہے۔ اسلئے اس صورت میں تلوار کی بیج درست ہوگی ۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ اس صورت میں تنہا تلواروں کی بیع درست ہے اور یہ باندی اور طوق کو ملا کر بیچنے کے مانند ہو گیا یعنی اگر باندی اور طوق کو ملا کر دو ہزار مثقال چاندی کے عوض بیچا اور بغیر قبضہ کئے عاقدین جدا ہو گئے تو طوق کی بیچ عقد صرف ہونے کی وجہ سے اگر چہ باطل ہو جائے گی مگر باندی کی بیچ درست ہو جائے گی ۔ کیونکہ باندی کو سپر د کر ناممکن ہے۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ تمام تفاصیل اس صورت میں ہیں جبکہ وہ چاندی جو الگ ہے یعنی جس کو شمن بنایا ہے اس چاندی سے زائد ہو جو مبیع یعنی تلوار سے لگی ہوئی ہے۔ جیسے مسئلہ مذکورہ میں تلوار سے لگی ہوئی چاندی پچاس درہم ہے اور شمن ایک سو درہم ہے تو یہ صورت جائز ہے کیونکہ جو چاندی مبیع کے ساتھ لگی ہوئی ہے وہ چاندی اپنے بقدر چاندی کے عوض ہو جائے گی۔ اور جو چاندی زائد ہے وہ مبیع کے عوض ہو جائے گی۔ اور اگر وہ چاندی جو شمن ہے اس چاندی کے برابر ہو جو مبیع کے ساتھ پیوستہ ہے یا اس سے کم ہو یا اس کی مقدار معلوم نہ ہو تو بیع جائز نہ ہوگی ۔ کیونکہ جس صورت میں شمن کی چاندی مبیع سے لگی ہوئی چاندی کے برابر ہے یا کم ہے تو ر بوالا زم آئے گا۔ برابر کی صورت میں تو اسلئے کہ چاندی، چاندی کے عوض ہوگئی اور مبیع یعنی تلوار بلا عوض رہی اور کم کی صورت میں تلوار اور کچھ چاندی بلاعوض رہے گی ۔ مثلا شمن کی چاندی دس تولہ ہے اور تلوار پر لگی ہوئی چاندی بارہ تولہ ہے تو دس تولہ، دس تولہ کے عوض ہوگئی اور تلوار اور دو تولہ چاندی بلاعوض رہی اور عقد بیع میں کسی چیز کا بلاعوض ہونا یہی رہوا ہے اسلئے ان دونوں صورتوں میں ربوا لا زم آئے گا اور اگر شمن کی چاندی کی مقدار معلوم نہ ہو تو اس صورت میں ربوا کا احتمال ہے کیونکہ یہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے کہ شمن کی چاندی اس چاندی سے زائد ہو جو بیع کے ساتھ پیوست ہے اور یہ بھی ہوسکتا ہے کہ اس کے برابر ہو اور یہ بھی ہوسکتا ہے کہ اس سے کم ہو۔ بہر حال اس صورت میں احتمال ربوا ہے اور احتمال ربوا چونکہ حقیقت ربوا کی طرح حرام ہے اسلئے یہ صورت بھی نا جائز ہوگی۔ وجهة الفساد الخ سے سوال کا جواب ہے۔
وال یہ ہے کہ شمن کی چاندی کی مقدارا اگر معلوم نہ ہو تو بیع جائز ہونی چاہئے ۔ کیونکہ اصل بیع کا جائز ہونا ہے اور مفسد وہ زیادتی ہے جو جوش سے خالی ہو۔ پس جب تک زیادتی کا علم یقینی طور پر نہ ہو اس وقت تک جواز کا حکم لگا یا جائے گا؟ جواب ... اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ثمن کی چاندی کی مقدار معلوم نہ ہونے کی صورت میں تین احتمال ہیں یا تو شمن کی چاندی اس چاندی سے زائد ہوگی ۔ جو بیع یعنی تلوار کے ساتھ لگی ہوئی ہے یا اس کے برابر ہوگی اور یا اس سے کم ہوگی ۔ پہلی صورت میں یعنی زائد ہونے کی صورت میں تو بیع جائز ہے اور باقی دوصورتوں میں یعنی برابر اور کم ہونے کی صورت میں بیج فاسد ہے پس شمن کی چاندی کی مقدار معلوم نہ ہونے کی صورت میں ایک اعتبار سے بیع جائز ہے اور دو اعتبار سے بیع فاسد ہے اور یہ بات روز روشن کی طرح عیاں ہے کہ دو کو ایک پر ترجیح حاصل ہوتی ہے۔ لہذا جہت فساد راجح ہو گی اور جب جہت فساد راجح ہے تو اس صورت میں بھی بیچ فاسد ہوگی۔
Book of Sarf. And if the buyer says explicitly: 'These fifty dirhams are the price of both the sword and the ornament,' even then it will be considered the price of the ornament only. It is as if the buyer said: 'Take from the price of both what is necessary to take possession.' Because the affairs of Muslims are, as far as possible, interpreted in terms of validity and correctness, and here it is possible to interpret it as valid. In such a way that the dual pronoun in 'min thamanihima' is taken to refer to singular, and it is said that although the buyer said 'min thamanihima', his intended meaning is 'min thaman al-hilyah', and it is permissible to use the dual to mean singular. Just as in Allah's statement 'yakhruju minhuma al-lu'lu' wa al-marjan', the dual pronoun 'minhuma' refers to the two seas, salt and fresh, but pearls and coral come only from the salt sea, not the fresh. From this it is known that the dual in 'minhuma' means singular. Similarly, in the story of Musa and Khidr (peace be upon them), Allah said 'nasiya hutahuma' – a dual form, whereas the forgetter of the fish at the meeting of the two seas was only the servant of Musa. Here too, the dual is used to mean singular. And as Allah said 'qad ujibat da'watukuma' while the intended meaning is only the prayer of Musa. And as the Messenger of Allah (may Allah bless him and give him peace) said to Malik ibn al-Huwairith and his cousin: 'idha safartuma fa adhdhina wa aqima' – meaning 'When you travel, give the call to prayer and establish the iqamah.' Here too, 'adhdhina' and 'aqima' are dual forms meaning singular because one person says the adhan and iqamah. And as Allah's statement 'ya ma'shara al-jinni wa al-insi alam ya'tikum rusulun minkum' – whereas according to Ahl al-Haqq, messengers are only from the human assembly, not from the jinn; so here 'minkum' (from you two) is used but only the human assembly is meant. So when using dual to mean singular is widespread and common, then considering the Muslim buyer's condition, his statement 'min thamanihima' will be interpreted as 'min thaman al-hilyah' because the apparent state of a Muslim is that he does what is permissible and does not commit what is impermissible. And the validity of the sale can only be in the case where the mentioned fifty dirhams are considered the price of the ornament. Therefore, even in the case of saying 'khudh hadhihi al-khamsina min thamanihima', these fifty dirhams will be counted as the price of the ornament, not as the price of both the ornament and the sword. Sahib al-Quduri says: In both mentioned cases – the case of the slave-girl and the necklace, and the case of the sword and the ornament – if mutual possession (qabd) did not take place in the session and the two contracting parties separated, then the contract becomes void (batil) with respect to the portion of the necklace and the ornament. Because in the portions of the necklace and the ornament, both countervalues are silver, so this contract is a sarf contract, and since the sarf contract becomes void by separation without possession, the contract becomes void for the necklace and ornament portion. And it becomes valid for the slave-girl portion. As for the matter of the sword: if the ornament is attached to the sword in such a way that separating the ornament from the sword without harm is not possible, then the sale of the sword also becomes void. Because in that case, delivery of the sword without harm is not possible, and when delivery of the sold item without harm is not possible, the sale becomes void. So in this case, the sale of the sword also becomes void. This is the reason that when separating the ornament from the sword without harm is not possible, selling only the sword is not valid, just as delivering a beam fixed in a ceiling without harm is not possible; therefore, selling such a beam is not valid. And if separating the ornament from the sword without harm is possible, then the sale of the sword becomes valid, and the sale of the ornament becomes void. Because in this case, delivering the sword (i.e., the sold item) without harm is possible, and when delivering the sold item without harm is possible, the sale is valid. Therefore, in this case, the sale of the sword is correct. This is the reason that in this case, selling only swords is correct, and it becomes like the case of selling the slave-girl and necklace together: if the slave-girl and necklace are sold together for two thousand mithqals of silver and the two parties separate without possession, then the sale of the necklace, being a sarf contract, becomes void, but the sale of the slave-girl remains valid because delivering the slave-girl is not impossible. Sahib al-Hidaya says: All these details are in a case where the silver that is separate (i.e., the price) exceeds the silver attached to the sold item (i.e., the sword). For example, in the mentioned case, the silver attached to the sword is fifty dirhams and the price is one hundred dirhams; this case is valid because the silver attached to the sold item goes in exchange for an equal amount of silver, and the excess silver goes in exchange for the sold item. But if the silver that is the price is equal to the silver attached to the sold item, or less, or its quantity is unknown, then the sale is not valid. Because in the case where the silver of the price is equal to or less than the silver attached to the sold item, riba becomes necessary. In the case of equality, because silver goes for silver and the sold item (the sword) remains without consideration; in the case of less, the sword and some silver remain unpaid. For example, if the silver of the price is ten tolas and the silver on the sword is twelve tolas, then ten tolas go for ten tolas, and the sword and two tolas of silver remain without consideration; in a sale contract, something being without consideration is riba. So in these two cases, riba becomes necessary. And if the quantity of the silver price is unknown, then there is a possibility of riba because it could be that the silver of the price is more than the silver attached to the sold item, or equal, or less. In any case, there is a possibility of riba, and since the possibility of riba is like the reality of riba in being prohibited, this case is also impermissible. 'Wajh al-fasad' etc. is the answer to the objection. And there is: If the quantity of the silver price is unknown, the sale should be valid because the original ruling for sale is validity, and the corrupting factor is an addition free from doubt. So as long as the addition is not certainly known, the ruling of validity should apply? The answer: The answer is that in the case of unknown quantity of the price silver, there are three possibilities: either the silver of the price is more than the silver attached to the sold item (the sword), or equal, or less. In the first case (more), the sale is valid; in the remaining two cases (equal or less), the sale is corrupt (fasid). So when the quantity is unknown, from one perspective the sale is valid and from two perspectives it is corrupt. And it is as clear as day that two have preference over one. Therefore, the aspect of corruption is preponderant, and when the aspect of corruption is preponderant, the sale is corrupt in this case as well.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) of a ruling from the matn of al-Hidayah regarding a specific sarf transaction: selling a sword with a silver ornament for silver dirhams. The author, Ashraf al-Hidayah, explains multiple sub-rulings in a structured manner. First, he addresses the buyer's ambiguous statement 'take these fifty dirhams from the price of both (the sword and ornament)'. Since a Muslim is presumed to act lawfully, the statement is interpreted as referring only to the price of the ornament, not the sword. This interpretation is supported by numerous Quranic and hadith examples where the dual form is used to mean singular, such as the verse 'yakhruju minhuma al-lu'lu' wa al-marjan' where 'minhuma' refers only to the salt sea. This is a Hanafi legal principle of interpreting ambiguous statements in a favorable manner when possible. Next, quoting Sahib al-Quduri, the text considers the scenario where the parties separate without having taken possession (qabd) in the session. Since the exchange of silver for silver is a sarf contract, it requires immediate possession; failure to do so before separation renders the contract batil (void) for the silver portion (the ornament) but valid for the non-sarf portion (the slave-girl or the sword if separable). However, if the ornament is so attached to the sword that separation would cause harm, the entire sale becomes void because delivery of the sword alone is impossible without also delivering the ornament, which is not a valid sarf transaction. Then, quoting Sahib al-Hidayah, the text adds a crucial condition: this ruling only applies when the silver price exceeds the silver attached to the sword. If the silver price is equal to or less than the attached silver, riba occurs because part of the silver is exchanged for itself without an additional non-sarf element. Similarly, if the quantity is unknown, the sale is corrupt (fasid) because the possibility of riba renders it invalid, following the principle that doubt of prohibition suffices to nullify the contract. The final objection and answer clarify why unknown quantity leads to corruption: the aspect of fasad outweighs the aspect of validity by a majority of probabilities (two out of three).

Key Terms
صرف (sarf): In this passage, sarf refers to the exchange of gold or silver for gold or silver, which is subject to strict rules of equality and immediate possession.
ثمن (thaman): The price (silver dirhams) paid for the sold item. In this context, it is silver that may be subject to sarf rules.
مبيع (mabi'): The sold item: the sword with silver ornament, a composite object.
قبض (qabd): Immediate possession or physical transfer of the items in the contract session.
ربا (riba): Usury or illegal increase in exchange; in this context, riba occurs when silver is exchanged for silver in unequal amounts or without immediate possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of selling a sword with a silver ornament for silver dirhams, when is the sale valid?
A) When the silver price exceeds the silver attached to the sword and immediate possession occurs.
B) When the silver price is equal to the silver attached to the sword and possession occurs later.
C) When the silver price is less than the attached silver.
D) It is always valid regardless of quantity.

Key Takeaway
The sale of a composite object containing silver for silver dirhams is valid only when the price exceeds the attached silver and immediate possession occurs; otherwise, it is invalid due to riba.

Ruling on Sarf Contract with Partial Payment and Separation
Ruling on a sarf contract with partial payment and separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a sarf contract when only partial possession is taken before separation, and identify the resulting joint ownership.

Core Idea
In a sarf contract, partial payment validates the corresponding share; the unpaid portion becomes void, creating joint ownership.

This passage addresses a common transaction in classical markets: selling a silver vessel for silver coins. The key issue is that when the buyer pays only part of the price and then the parties separate, the contract splits – valid for the paid portion, void for the unpaid portion. This ruling underscores the strict condition of immediate possession in sarf and results in joint ownership of the vessel.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type
Recognize that selling silver for silver is a sarf contract, which requires immediate possession of both counter-values before the parties separate.
Sarf is any exchange of gold for gold, silver for silver, or gold for silver.
2
Understand the condition
The essential condition for sarf validity is that both items must be exchanged immediately (qabd) before the session ends.
This is based on the hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ): 'Gold for gold, hand to hand...'
3
Apply the condition to partial payment
Since only part of the price was possessed, that part satisfies the condition for its proportion; the remainder fails due to lack of possession before separation.
The possession (qabd) of the price is actual physical taking.
4
Determine the result
The sale is valid for the paid portion (buyer gets that share of the vessel), void for the unpaid portion (seller retains that share). Thus the vessel becomes jointly owned.
Joint ownership is proportional to the amount paid.
5
Note the incidental corruption
The corruption (fasad) is not inherent from the start; the contract was initially valid for the whole. It becomes void later due to separation without full possession.
This is called 'fasad tari'' – incidental corruption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن باع إناء فضة ثم افترقا وقد قبض بعض ثمنه بطل البيع فيما لم يقبض وصح فيما قبض وكان الاناء مشتركا بينهما لانه صرف كله فصح فيما وجد شرطه وبطل فيما لم يوجد والفساد طار لانه يصح ثم يبطل
He said: And whoever sells a silver vessel, then the two separate after having taken possession of part of its price, the sale becomes void with respect to what was not taken possession, and valid with respect to what was taken possession, and the vessel becomes jointly owned between them. Because it is entirely a sarf (exchange of silver), so it is valid where its condition is found and void where it is not found, and the corruption is incidental, because it becomes valid then becomes void.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This is a concise ruling (matn) from al-Quduri or al-Hidayah on a specific scenario in sarf. A silver vessel is sold for silver dirhams. The parties separate after the buyer has paid only part of the price and taken possession of that part. Since the entire transaction involves silver for silver, it is a sarf contract, which requires immediate and full possession (qabd) before separation. Because partial possession was taken, the contract is valid for the portion paid (the paid silver is exchanged for a proportional share of the vessel), and void for the unpaid portion. As a result, the vessel becomes jointly owned: the seller retains ownership of the portion corresponding to the unpaid price, and the buyer owns the portion corresponding to the paid price. The ruling states 'the corruption is incidental' meaning the contract was initially valid for the entire item, but upon separation without full possession, the invalid part becomes void retroactively? Actually, the phrase 'al-fasad tar' indicates that the corruption (fasad) is not inherent from the start but occurs later due to the failure of condition. The explanation: the contract is valid at inception, but becomes void for the unpaid portion when the parties separate without having taken possession of that portion. This is a clear example of the strict requirement of immediate possession in sarf.

Key Terms
طارئ (tari'): Incidental; the corruption (fasad) arises after the contract's inception, not at the time of contracting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone sells a silver vessel for 100 dirhams, the buyer pays 40 dirhams and then they separate, what is the ruling?
A) The entire sale is void.
B) The sale is valid for 40% of the vessel and void for 60%.
C) The buyer must return the vessel and get his money back.
D) The sale is valid entirely because payment was started.

Key Takeaway
In sarf contracts, immediate possession of the entire price is a condition for validity; partial possession results in proportional validity and joint ownership.

Ruling on Selling a Silver-Adorned Sword in Sarf
Explains the ruling on selling a sword with silver when price silver differs from embedded silver

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a valid sarf sale when the sold item has silver attached and apply the Hanafi principle of preponderant probability to determine validity when the price silver quantity is unknown.

Core Idea
A sarf sale is valid only if the price silver exceeds the attached silver; uncertainty about the price amount invalidates the sale due to preponderant probability of riba.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling in Bay' al-Sarf (currency exchange) from the Hanafi school. When a non-ribawi item (e.g., a sword) has silver attached, and it is sold for silver, special rules apply to avoid riba. The key condition is that the price silver must be greater than the silver on the item. If the amount is unknown, the sale is invalid because the probability of riba outweighs the probability of validity. This ruling is based on a rational analysis of probabilities, unique to Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction type: Sarf
Recognize that selling a sword with silver for silver is a sarf contract because the countervalues include silver on both sides.
Sarf requires immediate possession, but here the focus is on equality.
2
Apply the condition for validity: Price silver must exceed attached silver
Check whether the price silver is greater than the silver on the sword. If yes, the sale is valid; if equal or less, it is invalid due to riba.
The analogy with bondmaid and necklace illustrates this.
3
Analyze the case of unknown price quantity
When the price silver quantity is unknown, consider the three possibilities. Since two out of three lead to invalidity, the invalid aspect is preponderant, making the sale fasid.
This is based on the Hanafi principle that probability of riba is treated like certainty in prohibition.
4
Respond to the objection of default permissibility
The objection that default permissibility should apply is answered by the preponderance of the invalid aspect. The ruling is not changed by the default because the evidence of probability overrides.
This shows the use of rational argument (istidlal) in Hanafi fiqh.

103 Kitab al-Sarf, Ashraf al-Bidaya, Volume 9: It is possible that in this case the sale is valid. Therefore, in this case the sale of the sword is correct. This is why in this case the sale of just the swords is correct, and it becomes like selling a bondmaid and a necklace together. That is, if a bondmaid and a necklace are sold together for two thousand mithqals of silver, and without taking possession the contracting parties separate, then the sale of the necklace, due to being a sarf contract, becomes invalid, but the sale of the bondmaid becomes correct because it is impossible to deliver the bondmaid [with the necklace?]. The author of al-Hidayah says: All these details apply when the silver that is separate, i.e., that which is made the price, is more than the silver that is attached to the sold item, i.e., the sword. For example, in the mentioned case, the silver attached to the sword is fifty dirhams, and the price is one hundred dirhams, then this case is valid because the silver attached to the sold item will be exchanged for its equivalent silver, and the extra silver will be in exchange for the sold item. And if that silver, which is the price, is equal to the silver affixed to the sold item, or less than it, or its quantity is unknown, then the sale is not valid. Because in the case where the price silver is equal to the attached silver or less, riba becomes incumbent. In the equal case, it is because silver is exchanged for silver, and the sold item, i.e., the sword, remains without compensation. In the lesser case, the sword and some silver will remain without compensation. For example, the price silver is ten tolas, and the silver on the sword is twelve tolas, then ten tolas are exchanged for ten tolas, and the sword and two tolas of silver remain without compensation. In a sale contract, something remaining without compensation is exactly riba. Therefore, in both cases riba becomes incumbent. And if the quantity of the price silver is unknown, then in that case there is a probability of riba, because it is possible that the price silver is more than the silver attached to the sold item, and it is also possible that it is equal, and it is also possible that it is less. In any case, in this situation there is a probability of riba, and since probability of riba is like the reality of riba in being prohibited, this case is also impermissible. This is the answer to the question from 'wajh al-fasad' etc. The answer is that if the quantity of the price silver is unknown, the sale should be valid because the original ruling of a sale is permissibility, and the invalidator is an addition that is free from [؟]. So as long as the addition is not known with certainty, the ruling of permissibility should be applied? The answer: The answer is that in the case of unknown quantity of the price silver, there are three probabilities: either the price silver is more than the silver attached to the sold item (i.e., the sword), or it is equal, or it is less. In the first case, i.e., when it is more, the sale is valid. In the remaining two cases, i.e., equal and less, the sale is fasid. So in the case of unknown quantity of the price silver, from one aspect the sale is valid, and from two aspects the sale is fasid. It is crystal clear that two have preference over one. Therefore, the aspect of invalidity prevails, and when the aspect of invalidity is preponderant, then in this case too the sale is fasid.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah deals with a nuanced ruling in Bay' al-Sarf (currency exchange) concerning the sale of an item that has silver attached to it, such as a sword with a silver handle or adornment, where the price is also silver. The passage type is a ruling with proof, specifically an expansion (sharh) of the Hidayah matn. The author begins by stating a general principle: when the price silver exceeds the silver on the sold item, the sale is valid. He illustrates this with an analogy: selling a bondmaid and a necklace together for silver. If the necklace is considered sarf (because it is silver), its sale becomes invalid if possession is not taken before separation, but the bondmaid's sale remains valid because she is not subject to sarf rules. Then the text explains the condition for the sword: the price silver must be greater than the silver on the sword. If equal or less, the sale is invalid due to riba, because part of the price silver would be exchanged for the sold item without a corresponding exchange. The author then addresses a potential objection: if the quantity of the price silver is unknown, some might argue that the sale should be valid because the original ruling of sales is permissibility. The answer is that when the quantity is unknown, there are three possibilities: more, equal, or less. Since equal and less lead to fasid, and these two outnumber the one valid possibility, the preponderant ruling is that the sale is fasid. This is based on the principle that probability of riba is treated like certainty in prohibiting the transaction. The Hanafi position is clear: to avoid riba, the price silver must be definitively more than the silver on the item.

Key Terms
صرف (sarf): A sale where the countervalues are both from the same ribawi genus (e.g., silver for silver). In Hanafi fiqh, it requires immediate possession (taqabud) before separation.
ربا (riba): Riba al-fadl: excess or inequality in exchange of ribawi items of the same genus. The passage considers unequal exchange of silver for silver as riba, even if one silver is attached to a non-ribawi item.
ثمن (thaman): The price paid in a sale. Here, it is silver, which is the countervalue in a sarf contract.
مبیع (mabi'): The sold item, here a sword with silver attached.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if a sword with silver is sold for silver and the price silver is equal to the attached silver, the sale is:
A) Sahih
B) Fasid due to riba
C) Fasid due to gharar
D) Batil

Key Takeaway
For a valid sarf sale of a non-ribawi item with attached silver, the price silver must exceed the attached silver; uncertainty about the price quantity invalidates the sale due to preponderant probability of riba.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Partial Validity in Sarf: Fasad Tari and Joint Ownership
Explains the ruling on sarf when only part of the price is received before separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the distinction between fasad asli and fasad tari to determine the validity of a sarf contract with partial payment before separation, and state the resulting joint ownership.

Core Idea
In sarf, supervening invalidity (fasad tari) does not spread; only the unpaid portion is invalid.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling in bay' al-sarf when the parties separate after only partial payment. The ruling hinges on a subtle but crucial distinction between original invalidity (fasad asli) and supervening invalidity (fasad tari). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the condition of mutual possession (taqabud) in currency and precious metal exchanges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Ruling
Read the matn: someone sells a silver vessel, they separate after the seller has received only part of the price. The ruling is that the sale is valid for the paid portion and invalid for the unpaid portion. The vessel becomes jointly owned proportionally.
Note the split: partial validity, not total validity or total invalidity.
2
Understand the Distinction Between Fasad Asli and Fasad Tari
The text explains that the invalidity here is 'fasad tari' because the contract was initially valid and became invalid only due to separation without full possession. In contrast, 'fasad asli' would invalidate the entire contract from inception.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school and critical for sarf.
3
Apply the Principle to the Case
Since the condition of taqabud (mutual possession before separation) is a condition for the continuation of validity (baqa al-jawaz), its absence only nullifies the unpaid portion. The paid portion met the condition and remains valid.
Thus the invalidity is localized to the missing possession.
4
Derive the Consequence: Joint Ownership
Because the buyer paid part of the price, he owns a corresponding share of the vessel. The seller retains ownership of the unpaid share. They become joint owners (shirkah) in proportion to payment.
Example: 50 dirhams paid out of 100 → half ownership each.
5
Consider a Third-Party Claim on Part of the Vessel
If a third party lawfully claims part of the vessel, the buyer has an option: either keep the remainder at a proportional price or return the whole because the joint ownership is a defect.
This shows that the partnership is treated as a defect (ayb).

ان کے بارے میں ضابطہ یہ ہے کہ اگر ابتدائے عقد میں فساد ہو تو وہ فساد اصلی کہلائے گا اور اگر عقد صحیح ہو کر منعقد ہوا اور پھر فساد پیدا ہوا تو یہ فساد طاری کہلاتا ہے اور ان دونوں کا حکم یہ ہے کہ فساد اصلی کی صورت میں پورا عقد فاسد ہو جاتا ہے ۔ اور فساد طاری کی صورت میں پورا عقد فاسد نہیں ہوتا بلکہ بقد رمفسد فاسد ہوتا ہے اور یہاں ہر نصف شمن پر قبضہ نہ کرنے کی وجہ سے جو فساد پیدا ہوا ہے یہ فساد طاری ہے کیونکہ عقد صرف میں جدا ہونے سے پہلے تقابض کی شرط ، جواز عقد کے بقاء کی شرط ہے۔ انعقاد عقد کی شرط نہیں ہے۔ یعنی عقد صرف
He sold a silver vessel, then both separated while he had taken possession of some of the price: the sale is invalid for that which he did not take possession, and valid for that which he took possession. And the vessel becomes shared between them because it is all a sarf [contract]. So it is valid where its condition is found, and invalid where it is not found. And the invalidity is 'tari' (supervening) because it becomes valid then invalidates by separation, so it does not spread. And if some part of the vessel is claimed [by a third party], then the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he may take the remainder for its share of the price, and if he wishes, he may return it because the partnership is a defect in the vessel.

Translation: And if someone sold a silver vessel, then both separated while the seller had taken possession of some of the price, then the sale becomes invalid for that part on which he did not take possession, and valid for that part on which he took possession. And the vessel will be shared between the seller and the buyer because the entire contract is sarf. So in whatever portion the condition of sarf is found, the contract is valid; and in whatever portion it is not found, it is invalid. And because the invalidity is supervening (tari) — since the contract becomes valid then later becomes invalid due to separation — therefore the invalidity does not spread. And if some part of the vessel is lawfully claimed, then the buyer has the option: if he wishes, he may take the remainder for its share of the price, and if he wishes, he may return it because the occurrence of partnership is a defect in the vessel.

Explanation of the case: A person sold a silver vessel, then the parties separated while before separation the buyer had paid a portion of the price. So to the extent that he paid the price, the sale is valid; and to the extent he did not pay, it is invalid. And the vessel will be shared between the seller and the buyer. For example, a vessel weighing one hundred dirhams was bought for one hundred dirhams, then the buyer only paid fifty dirhams and did not pay the other fifty, then both separated. So for fifty dirhams' worth, the sale of the vessel is valid, and for fifty it is invalid. And since the buyer paid half the price (fifty dirhams), he becomes owner of half the vessel, and the seller becomes owner of the other half. The proof is that this entire contract is a sarf contract, and the condition for the validity of sarf is mutual possession (taqabud), i.e., taking possession of both countervalues before separation. So in whatever portion this condition is met, the contract is valid; and in whatever portion it is not met, the contract is invalid.

And from 'walfasadu tar' etc. is the answer to a question.
The question is: When due to lack of possession, the sale of half the vessel became invalid, shouldn't this invalidity spread to the entire sale of the vessel, making the entire sale invalid instead of only half?
The answer: The answer is that invalidity is of two types: supervening invalidity (fasad tari) and original invalidity (fasad asli). The rule regarding them is that if the invalidity exists at the inception of the contract, it is called original invalidity; and if the contract was validly concluded and then invalidity occurs, it is called supervening invalidity. Their ruling is that in the case of original invalidity, the entire contract becomes invalid; and in the case of supervening invalidity, the entire contract does not become invalid, but rather only the amount of the invalid part becomes invalid. Here, the invalidity that occurred due to not taking possession of half the price is supervening invalidity because in a sarf contract, the condition of mutual possession before separation is a condition for the continuation of validity (baqa al-jawaz), not a condition for the initial conclusion (in'aqad) of the contract. That is, sarf...
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah addresses a key ruling in Bay' al-Sarf when payment is partial and the parties separate before full mutual possession. The passage type is a ruling with proof, specifically a sharh expansion of a matn from al-Hidayah. The matn states: if someone sells a silver vessel and they separate after the seller has received only part of the price, the sale is invalid for the unpaid portion but valid for the paid portion, and the vessel becomes jointly owned in proportion to payment. The reason is that the entire contract is sarf, and the condition for validity (shart al-jawaz) of sarf is mutual possession (taqabud) before separation. This condition applies to each part of the contract separately. The commentator then explains that the invalidity here is 'fasad tari' (supervening invalidity), which does not spread to the entire contract, unlike 'fasad asli' (original invalidity) which invalidates the whole. The difference is crucial: in sarf, the contract is valid at inception but becomes invalid if possession is not taken before separation; the invalidity attaches only to the portion where the condition is missing. The passage also discusses a scenario where a third party claims part of the vessel, giving the buyer an option to either keep the rest at a proportional price or return the whole due to the partnership defect. This highlights the Hanafi principle that partial validity in sarf is possible, and supervening invalidity is localized.

Key Terms
صرف (sarf): A contract exchanging ribawi items (gold, silver, or currency) where immediate possession is required for validity.
فساد طاری (fasad tari): Supervening invalidity: a contract that is initially valid but becomes invalid due to a later event. It does not spread to the entire contract.
تقابض (taqabud): Mutual possession: both parties take physical possession of their respective countervalues.
شرکت (shirkah): Joint ownership: here, the silver vessel becomes jointly owned by the seller and buyer in proportion to payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if a silver vessel is sold for 100 dirhams, the buyer pays 50 dirhams, and then the parties separate without taking possession of the remaining 50 dirhams, what is the ruling?
A) The entire sale is batil (invalid).
B) The entire sale is sahih (valid) because partial payment suffices.
C) The sale is sahih for half the vessel and batil for the other half; the vessel is jointly owned.
D) The sale is suspended until full payment.

Key Takeaway
In sarf, supervening invalidity (fasad tari) does not spread; only the unpaid portion is invalid, resulting in joint ownership proportional to payment.

Partial Payment and Supervening Invalidity in Sarf (Silver Vessel Sale)
Explaining partial validity and non-spread of supervening invalidity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the distinction between fasad asli and fasad tari to determine the validity of partial payment in a sarf contract and identify when the buyer retains an option.

Core Idea
In sarf, missing possession before separation voids only the unpaid portion—supervening invalidity does not spread.

Consider buying a silver vessel with silver coins. The Hanafi school treats this as a sarf contract requiring immediate possession before parting. This Hidayah passage explores a tricky case: the buyer pays only part of the price before the parties separate. The ruling is nuanced—only the unpaid portion becomes void, and the vessel becomes joint property. The text also addresses objections about contract division and a separate scenario where a third party claims part of the vessel, granting the buyer an option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract as sarf and its ruling condition
Recognize that exchanging a silver vessel for silver is a sarf contract, which requires immediate possession (taqabud) of both counter-values before the parties separate for the contract to remain valid.
Sarf is defined as the exchange of gold and silver, including vessels and coins.
2
Apply partial payment scenario
If the buyer pays only part of the price before separation, determine that the condition of taqabud is fulfilled for the paid portion but not the unpaid. Conclude that the sale is valid (sahih) for the paid portion and void (batil) for the unpaid, and the vessel becomes joint property proportionally.
Example: 100 dirham vessel, 50 paid → buyer owns half, seller owns half.
3
Distinguish fasad asli vs fasad tari
The invalidity here is supervening (fasad tari) because the contract was valid at inception and became invalid only upon separation without full possession. Unlike original invalidity (fasad asli) which voids the whole contract, fasad tari only affects the part where the condition is missing.
The analogy of two slaves (one dies before possession) illustrates this principle.
4
Address the tafriq al-safqah objection
Normally, division of a single transaction (tafriq al-safqah) gives the buyer an option to annul the entire sale. Here, the buyer caused the division by not paying the full price, so his option is forfeited.
The buyer's own negligence eliminates the right to complain about division.
5
Analyze istihqaq scenario
If a third party claims part of the vessel after the sale (istihqaq), the buyer has an option: he may take the remaining portion at a proportionate price or return it. This is because co-ownership is a defect, and the defect was not caused by the buyer.
This contrasts with the earlier case where the buyer's own action caused the division.

The condition of separation does not cause [invalidity] to spread. And if part of the vessel is claimed by a third party, the buyer has an option: if he wishes, he may take the remainder at its proportionate price, and if he wishes, he may return it, because co-ownership in a vessel is a defect. Translation: And if someone sold a silver vessel, then the two separated while the seller had taken possession of part of the price, then the sale becomes void for the portion on which the seller did not take possession, and valid for the portion on which he did take possession, and the vessel becomes jointly owned between the seller and the buyer because this entire contract is a sarf contract. So to the extent that the condition of sarf was fulfilled, the contract is valid; to the extent it was not fulfilled, it is void. And because the invalidity is supervening, because the contract was valid and then became void due to separation, therefore the invalidity does not spread. And if part of the vessel is claimed by a third party, the buyer has an option: he may take the remainder at its proportionate price or return it, because co-ownership in the vessel is a defect. Explanation: The issue is that a person sold a silver vessel, then the parties separated while the buyer had paid part of the price before separation. So to the extent of the price paid, the sale is valid; to the extent not paid, it is void. And the vessel becomes jointly owned between seller and buyer. For example, a vessel weighing 100 dirhams was bought for 100 dirhams, then the buyer paid only 50 dirhams and the remaining 50 not, then they separated. So the sale of the vessel to the extent of 50 dirhams is valid, and to the extent of 50 is void. Since the buyer paid half the price, he owns half the vessel, and the seller owns the other half. Proof: Because this entire contract is a sarf contract, and the condition for the permissibility of the sarf contract is possession before separation. So to the extent that this condition is found, the contract is valid; to the extent it is not found, it is void. And 'wa al-fasad tari...' etc. is an answer to a question. The question: When the sale of half the vessel became invalid due to lack of possession, why does this invalidity not spread to the entire vessel? The answer: There are two types of invalidity: original and supervening. If the invalidity is present at the inception of the contract, it is original; if the contract was validly concluded and then invalidity occurs, it is supervening. The rule is that in the case of original invalidity, the entire contract becomes invalid; in the case of supervening invalidity, only the part affected becomes invalid. Here, the invalidity is supervening because in sarf, the condition of possession before separation is a condition for the continuation of the contract's validity, not for its initial validity. So the contract was validly concluded, but when separation occurred without possession, invalidity arose. Since it is supervening, it does not spread. An example: A person sold two slaves, then before the buyer took possession, one died. The sale of the living slave remains valid, and the sale of the dead slave becomes void. Objection: In this situation, the buyer faces division of the single transaction. Normally in division of the single transaction, the buyer has an option. Here, the buyer is not given an option. Answer: The division is caused by the buyer's own action, because if he had paid the full price before separation, there would be no division. Since he is at fault, his right to option is forfeited. However, if part of the vessel is claimed by a third party, then the buyer has an option: he may take the remainder at its proportionate price or return it, because co-ownership is a defect, and the defect is not due to the buyer's action. Therefore, he has the option.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a ruling from the Hidayah on the contract of sarf (exchange of gold/silver). The case involves a silver vessel sold for silver, which is considered a sarf contract. The key condition in sarf is immediate possession (taqabud) of both counter-values before the parties separate. Here, the buyer paid only part of the price before separation. The author first states the ruling: the sale is valid for the portion paid and void for the unpaid portion, because the condition of possession was partially fulfilled. The vessel becomes joint property. The author then addresses a potential objection: why does the invalidity of the unpaid portion not spread to the valid portion? He explains the difference between fasad asli (original invalidity) and fasad tari (supervening invalidity). Since the contract was initially valid but became invalid later due to separation without full possession, it is supervening invalidity, which does not spread beyond the affected part. He gives the analogy of selling two slaves where one dies before possession. Then he addresses another objection about tafriq al-safqah (division of the single transaction). Normally, when a transaction is split, the buyer has an option. But here, the buyer caused the division by not paying the full price, so his option is forfeited. Finally, he discusses a different scenario: if part of the vessel is claimed by a third party (istihqaq), then the buyer does have an option because co-ownership is a defect, and the defect is not due to the buyer's fault. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi principle that in sarf, the condition of immediate possession is for the continuity of the contract, not its formation, and that supervening invalidity is limited.

Key Terms
صرف (sarf): Exchange of gold and silver (currency) where immediate possession before separation is required.
تقابض (taqabud): Mutual possession or hand-to-hand delivery of both counter-values before separation of the parties.
فساد طاری (fasad tari): Supervening invalidity: a defect that occurs after the contract was validly concluded, affecting only the part where the defect arises.
فساد اصلی (fasad asli): Original invalidity: a defect present at the time of contracting, rendering the entire contract void.
تفریق صفقہ (tafriq al-safqah): Division of a single transaction into multiple parts, which normally gives the buyer an option to annul or accept.
استحقاق (istihqaq): A claim by a third party to ownership of part of the sold item, proven after the sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi view, if a sarf contract is partially executed (partial payment) before separation, what is the ruling on the unpaid portion?
A) The entire sale becomes void.
B) The sale remains valid but must be completed later.
C) The sale is void only for the unpaid portion; the paid portion remains valid.
D) The sale is suspended until full payment is made.

Key Takeaway
Supervening invalidity (fasad tari) in a sarf contract only affects the portion where the condition of immediate possession is missing; it does not spread to the valid portion.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner

Sale of Silver Lump with Partial Third-Party Claim
Ruling on istihqaq of part of a silver lump; no option for buyer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when part of a sold silver lump is claimed by a third party and explain why the buyer has no option.

Core Idea
Splitting a silver lump causes no harm; thus co-ownership is not a defect and the buyer has no option.

This lesson introduces a key distinction in Hanafi sale law: when a sold item is partially claimed by a third party, the buyer's rights depend on whether splitting harms the item's utility. For a homogeneous silver lump, splitting is harmless, so the buyer must accept the remainder at a proportionate price with no option to reject. This ruling contrasts with crafted items like vessels, where splitting destroys value and grants an option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sold item and the event
The seller sold a lump of refined silver (qit'at naqrah). After the sale, a third party proved ownership of part of that lump.
The item is not a crafted vessel but a raw lump.
2
State the ruling for the buyer
The buyer takes the remaining part of the lump at a price proportionate to its share of the total sale price. The buyer has no option to return the remainder or reject the transaction.
The sale remains binding on the buyer.
3
Identify the legal cause ('illa)
The reason ( 'illa) is that splitting (tab'id) does not harm the buyer. A silver lump can be divided without loss of value or utility.
Harm (darar) is the criterion for giving an option.
4
Contrast with a crafted vessel
If the item were a vessel, splitting would harm the utility, making co-ownership a defect. There, the buyer would have an option to return the whole item.
The nature of the item determines whether splitting is harmful.
5
Apply the principle
Since splitting is not harmful, co-ownership is not considered a defect. Therefore, the buyer has no option (khiyar).
The ruling is specific to homogeneous items like silver lumps.

و من باع قطعة نقرة ثم استحق بعضها اخذ ما بقى بحصته ولا خيار له لانه لا يضره التبعيض ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے چاندی کا ایک ٹکڑا فروخت کیا پھر اس کا ایک مستحق ہو گیا تو مابقی کو اس کے حصہ یمن کے عوض لے لے گا اور اس کو اختیار بھی نہ ہوگا کیونکہ اس کو ٹکڑے کرنا کچھ مضر نہیں ہے۔ تشریح مسئلہ، اگر کسی نے گلائی ہوئی چاندی کا ٹکڑا فروخت کیا پھر عاقدین کے علاوہ تیسرے آدمی نے اس کے ایک حصہ میں اپنا استحقاق ثابت کر دیا تو جس قدر چاندی کا ٹکڑا غیر مستحق رہا مشتری اس کو اس کے حصہ یمن کے عوض لے لے اور مشتری کو لینے اور نہ لینے کا اختیار بھی نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس کو ٹکڑے ٹکڑے کرنا مضر نہیں ہے۔ اور جب ٹکڑے کرنا مضر نہیں ہے تو اس میں شرکت ، عیب شمار نہ ہوگا اور جب شرکت عیب نہیں ہے تو مشتری کو اختیار بھی حاصل نہ ہوگا۔
And whoever sold a piece of silver (naqrah), then part of it was claimed by a third party, he takes the remainder at its proportionate price, and he has no option because splitting does not harm him. Translation: And if someone sold a silver lump, then part of it was claimed by a third party, he takes the remainder at its proportionate price, and he has no option because splitting does not harm him. Explanation: If someone sold a lump of refined silver, then a third party proved his claim to part of it, the buyer takes the remaining silver at its proportionate price, and he has no option to take or leave, because splitting is not harmful. And when splitting is not harmful, co-ownership is not considered a defect, and when co-ownership is not a defect, the buyer has no option.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This is a short ruling contrasting with the previous case. The author, Marghinani, states that if a lump of silver (as opposed to a vessel) is sold and part is claimed by a third party, the buyer does not have an option to return the rest; he must take the remainder at a proportionate price. The reason is that splitting a lump into pieces does not cause harm to the buyer, unlike a vessel where co-ownership is a defect. This distinction is based on the utility of the item: a vessel's value is diminished if partially owned, but a lump of silver remains easily divisible without loss of value. The commentary emphasizes that since splitting is not harmful, the resulting co-ownership is not considered a defect, and therefore no option (khiyar) arises. This ruling is specific to the Hanafi school and illustrates the principle that the buyer's option in case of istihqaq depends on whether the remaining part is equally beneficial.

Key Terms
قطعة نقرة (qit'at naqrah): A lump of refined silver, as opposed to a crafted item like a vessel.
تبعبیض (tab'id): Dividing an item into parts; here, it is not harmful for a silver lump.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer may return the entire item.
B) The buyer takes the remainder at proportionate cost with no option to reject.
C) The buyer may choose to take the remainder or return it.
D) The sale becomes void entirely.

Key Takeaway
For a silver lump, splitting is not harmful, so the buyer must accept the remainder at proportionate price with no option; contrast with crafted items where splitting gives an option.

Mixed Currency Exchange: Hanafi Permissibility vs Shafi'i Prohibition
Hanafi vs Shafi'i dispute on mixed sarf transaction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits selling two dirhams and one dinar for one dirham and two dinars, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the principle of whole-to-whole exchange.

Core Idea
Mixed sarf is valid as a whole exchange; total value matters, not exact pairing.

This lesson examines a classic dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over a mixed currency transaction: selling two dirhams and one dinar for one dirham and two dinars. The Hanafi school permits it based on the principle of muqābala bil-jumla (whole-to-whole exchange), while the Shafi'i school prohibits it, requiring exact matching of each currency type. Understanding this difference illuminates the role of istihsan in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Transaction and the Dispute
State the exact transaction: selling a bundle of two dirhams and one dinar for a bundle of one dirham and two dinars. Recognize that this is a sarf (currency exchange) involving two different jins (silver and gold).
The dispute is about whether the mixing of types is permissible.
2
Analyze the Hanafi Position and its Dalil
The Hanafi ruling is jāʾiz (permissible). The dalil is the principle of muqābala bil-jumla: the transaction is considered as a single exchange of total values, not a set of individual exchanges. The net quantity of each type is balanced (2-1 dirhams, 1-2 dinars overall equivalent).
This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) allowing flexibility.
3
Examine the Shafi'i Objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that sarf requires immediate possession and exact matching of each type to avoid riba. Mixing types leads to uncertainty (gharar) because the exchange rate between dirhams and dinars is not fixed in the contract.
The Shafi'i view is that each type must be exchanged separately.
4
Understand the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response is that the exchange is valid by istihsan. The transaction is a single contract with a single price; allocation to each type is not specified. The principle of division on a common basis (inqisām ʿalā al-shuyūʿ) avoids riba by ensuring overall equivalence.
Two analogies support the Shafi'i argument but the Hanafi position relies on a holistic view.
5
Note the Thamarah al-Khilaf and Tarjih
Practical consequence: Hanafi allows such mixed exchanges; Shafi'i requires separate contracts. The Hanafi position is muftā bihi in the school, meaning it is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال و من باع در همین و دینا را بدرهم و دینارين جاز البيع وجعل كل جنس منهما بخلافه وقال زفرو الشافعي لا يجوز وعلى هذا الخلاف اذا باع كرشعير وكر حنطة بكرى حنطة وكرى شعير لهما ان في الصرف الى خلاف الجنس تغيير تصرفه لانه قابل الجملة بالجملة ومن قضيته الانقسام على الشيوع لا على التعيين والتغيير لا يجوز وان كان فيه تصحيح التصرف كما اذا اشترى قلبا بعشرة وثوبا بعشرة ثم باعهما مرابحة لايجوزوان امكن صرف الربح الى الثوب وكذا اذا اشترى عبدا بالف درهم ثم باعه قبل نقد الثمن من البائع مع عبد أخر بالف وخمس مائة لا يجوز فى المشترى بالف و ان امكن تصحيحه بصرف
He said: And whoever sold two dirhams and a dinar for one dirham and two dinars, the sale is permissible, and he makes each type opposite to its counterpart. And Abu Zufr? al-Shafi'i said it is not permissible. And on this difference, if he sold a kurr of barley and a kurr of wheat for two kurr of wheat and two kurr of barley, then in his view [Shafi'i's], [the exchange] to a different type changes his transaction because he considered the whole against the whole. And the consequence is division on a common basis, not on specification. And change is not permissible, even if it involves correcting the transaction, as when he bought a heart for ten and a cloth for ten, then sold them at profit, it is not permissible even if it is possible to attribute the profit to the cloth. And similarly, if he bought a slave for a thousand dirhams, then sold him before paying the price to the seller along with another slave for one thousand five hundred, it is not permissible for the one bought for a thousand, even if it is possible to correct it by [attributing the price] to the slave [?].
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi position (Imam Muhammad or Abu Hanifa via 'qāla') permits selling two dirhams and one dinar for one dirham and two dinars. The reasoning is that the transaction is considered as a whole exchange (jimala) where the total value of silver and gold is balanced. The Hanafi school applies the principle of muqābala bil-jumla (exchange of the total), so the exact pairing of each type is not required. Imam al-Shafi'i, on the other hand, considers this invalid because it conflates different types (dirhams and dinars) and argues that each type must be exchanged for its own counterpart separately (e.g., dirhams for dirhams, dinars for dinars) to avoid riba. The text extends the dispute to grains: selling a kurr of barley and a kurr of wheat for two kurr of wheat and two kurr of barley. Shafi'i's view is that this changes the nature of the transaction. The Hanafi respond by stating that the whole-to-whole exchange is permissible, and the division occurs on a common basis (shuyu'). The text gives two analogies: (1) buying a heart and a cloth for separate prices and selling them together at profit is not permissible even if profit could be attributed to the cloth; (2) buying a slave for 1000 dirhams and then re-selling him with another slave before paying the original price is also not permissible even if correction is possible. These analogies support the Shafi'i argument, but the Hanafi position is that in the original case of mixed sarf, the transaction is valid because it is a single exchange of total values, not a set of individual exchanges. The text ends mid-sentence; the author likely continues to defend the Hanafi view. This passage illustrates the subtlety of Hanafi usul in applying istihsan to permit transactions that resemble riba but are actually valid when considered holistically.

Key Terms
صرف (sarf): Exchange of gold and silver (currency) where immediate possession is required.
الجملة بالجملة (al-jumla bil-jumla): Wholesale exchange, where the total value of one bundle is exchanged for the total value of another without itemized pairing.
انقسام على الشيوع (inqisām ʿalā al-shuyūʿ): Division on a common basis, i.e., each item in the bundle is considered as an undivided share of the whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Impermissible because it involves riba.
C) Permissible only if each type is specified.
D) Impermissible because the amounts are not equal.

Key Takeaway
Mixed currency exchange is valid in the Hanafi school when total values are balanced, based on the principle of whole-to-whole exchange (muqābala bil-jumla).

Partial Claim on a Silver Piece: Buyer Takes Remainder Without Option
Ruling on partial claim in a sale of fungible goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when part of a sold silver piece is claimed by a third party and explain the reasoning based on the absence of harm from partition.

Core Idea
If partitioning the sold silver inflicts no harm, the buyer must accept the unclaimed portion without option.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in the sale of fungible goods: a piece of silver (naqara) is sold, and later a third party proves ownership of part of it. The question is whether the buyer can reject the remaining portion. The Hanafi school clarifies that the buyer must take the remainder proportionally and has no option to rescind, because dividing the silver does not cause harm. Understanding this ruling reveals a key principle: options in sale (khiyar) arise only when there is detriment; if partition is harmless, no option is granted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on the remainder
Read the matn: after partial claim, the buyer 'takes what remains based on its share of the price.' Determine what happens to the unclaimed portion.
The buyer does not get a discount or return; he pays a proportion of the original price.
2
Ascertain the buyer's option (khiyar)
The matn explicitly says 'he has no option.' Confirm that the buyer cannot reject the remainder even if he prefers.
This contrasts with cases where a defect gives an option; here there is no option.
3
Extract the ta'lil (legal cause)
The reason given: 'because partitioning does not harm him.' Identify what property of silver makes partition harmless.
Silver is fungible: each part is like the whole; splitting does not reduce utility or value per unit.
4
Apply the principle to other items
Consider if the same ruling applies to other fungibles (e.g., gold, wheat) vs. unique items (e.g., a sword). The ta'lil determines the scope.
If partition harms the item (e.g., a carpet), the buyer would have an option.
5
Note the cross-madhhab perspective
The Hanafi ruling is specific; other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may grant an option when ownership is partially defeated. Understand the point of ikhtilaf.
This shows how different assessments of 'harm' affect rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و من باع قطعة نقرة ثم استحق بعضها اخذ ما بقى بحصته ولا خيار له لانه لا يضره التبعيض ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے چاندی کا ایک ٹکڑا فروخت کیا پھر اس کا ایک مستحق ہو گیا تو مابقی کو اس کے حصہ یمن کے عوض لے لے گا اور اس کو اختیار بھی نہ ہوگا کیونکہ اس کو ٹکڑے کرنا کچھ مضر نہیں ہے۔ تشریح مسئلہ، اگر کسی نے گلائی ہوئی چاندی کا ٹکڑا فروخت کیا پھر عاقدین کے علاوہ تیسرے آدمی نے اس کے ایک حصہ میں اپنا استحقاق ثابت کر دیا تو جس قدر چاندی کا ٹکڑا غیر مستحق رہا مشتری اس کو اس کے حصہ یمن کے عوض لے لے اور مشتری کو لینے اور نہ لینے کا اختیار بھی نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس کو ٹکڑے ٹکڑے کرنا مضر نہیں ہے۔ اور جب ٹکڑے کرنا مضر نہیں ہے تو اس میں شرکت ، عیب شمار نہ ہوگا اور جب شرکت عیب نہیں ہے تو مشتری کو اختیار بھی حاصل نہ ہوگا۔
And whoever sells a piece of silver, then part of it is claimed (by a third party), he takes what remains based on its share of the price, and he has no option, because partitioning does not harm him. Translation: And if someone sold a piece of silver, then part of it became claimed, he takes the remainder in exchange for its share of the price, and he has no option, because cutting it is not harmful. Explanation: If someone sold a wrought silver piece, then a third person proved his claim in part of it, the customer takes the unclaimed portion in exchange for its share of the price, and he has no option to take or leave, because cutting it into pieces is not harmful. And when cutting is not harmful, partnership (in ownership) is not considered a defect, and when partnership is not a defect, the customer does not have an option.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Kitab al-Sarf on partial claim in a sale of silver. The matn states that if a piece of silver is sold and later part of it is claimed by a third party (i.e., the seller did not own it), the buyer takes the remaining unclaimed portion in proportion to its share of the total price, and he has no option to rescind the sale. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that cutting the silver piece does not harm the buyer because silver is fungible and divisible; thus the partial claim does not create a defect. The Urdu sharh by Ashraf al-Hadaya elaborates that the buyer must take the remainder and has no khiyar because the silver is of a type where splitting is not detrimental. The buyer's option is only established for defects that cause harm, and here partition is not harmful. This ruling applies specifically to silver and similar fungibles where partial claim does not affect utility.

Key Terms
قطعة نقرة (qit'at naqara): A piece of pure silver, often molded or wrought, not coined.
استحقاق (istihqaq): Claim of ownership by a third party proven against the seller.
تبعيد (tab'id): Partition or splitting of the sold item into parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The buyer must return the unclaimed portion to the seller.
D) The buyer has the choice to take or leave the unclaimed portion.

Key Takeaway
For fungible goods like silver, a partial claim does not give the buyer an option to reject the remainder because partition is not harmful.

Selling Mixed Currencies: Hanafi Permission via Reinterpretation
Inter-madhhab dispute with counter-analogies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on exchanging mixed currencies with unequal quantities and evaluate the Shafi'i/Zufar counter-argument.

Core Idea
In a bundle sale of dirhams and dinars, the Hanafi school saves the contract from riba by assigning each type to its opposite.

This passage presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute. The core question: when a person sells a mixed bag of dirhams and dinars for another mixed bag with different counts, is the sale valid? The Hanafi school, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, says yes—by reinterpreting the consideration. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify that the sale is valid (ja'iz) and recognize the reinterpretation: each dirham is considered exchanged for a dinar, and vice versa.
The Hanafi view avoids riba by ensuring that equal amounts of the same currency are not directly exchanged with excess.
2
Identify the opposing view
Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) and al-Shafi'i hold the sale invalid because it changes the parties' disposition (taghyir al-tasarruf).
This is an intra-Hanafi disagreement as well, as Zufar splits from the majority.
3
Follow the opponents' proof
The opponents argue that the contract was for a total against a total, requiring division upon joint ownership; any reallocation is an impermissible change.
They support this by analogies where such reallocation is not accepted (e.g., murabaha sale of a necklace and garment).
4
Analyze the counter-analogies
The passage lists examples (necklace/garment, slave sales, ambiguous sale, dirham/garment without possession) where a similar 'correction' is rejected, but Hanafi holds the currency case is distinct due to riba avoidance.
These analogies show the limits of istihsan: the currency case is saved by necessity, but other contexts are not.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Hanafi, the contract is valid and parties must exchange as per the reinterpretation; under Shafi'i, the contract is void and items must be returned.
This affects whether the transaction can proceed without sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال و من باع درهمين و دينارا بدرهم و دينارين جاز البيع وجعل كل جنس منهما بخلافه وقال زفرو الشافعي لا يجوز وعلى هذا الخلاف اذا باع كر شعير وكر حنطة بكرى حنطة وكرى شعير لهما ان في الصرف الى خلاف الجنس تغيير تصرفه لانه قابل الجملة بالجملة ومن قضيته الانقسام على الشيوع لا على التعيين والتغيير لا يجوز وان كان فيه تصحيح التصرف كما اذا اشترى قلبا بعشرة وثوبا بعشرة ثم باعهما مرابحة لايجوزوان امكن صرف الربح الى الثوب وكذا اذا اشترى عبدا بالف درهم ثم باعه قبل نقد الثمن من البائع مع عبد آخر بالف وخمس مائة لا يجوز فى المشترى بالف وان امكن تصحيحه بصرف الالف اليه وكذا اذا جمع بين عبده وعبد غيره وقال بعتك احدهما لا يجوزو ان امكن تصحيحه بصرفه الى عبده وكذا اذا باع درهما وثوبا بدرهم وثوب وافترقا من غير قبض فسد العقد في الدرهمين ولا يصرف الدرهم الى الثوب لما ذكرنا
He said: And whoever sells two dirhams and a dinar for one dirham and two dinars, the sale is valid, and each type (of currency) is placed in exchange for the opposite type. And Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: It is not valid. And the same dispute applies when one sells a kor of barley and a kor of wheat for two kors of wheat and two kors of barley. Their (Zufar and Shafi'i's) reasoning is that turning (the consideration) to the opposite type changes the disposition, because he presented the total against the total, and its requirement is division upon joint ownership, not upon specification, and changing (the disposition) is not permissible, even if it corrects the transaction. For example, if someone buys a necklace for ten and a garment for ten, then sells them both at cost (murabaha), it is not valid, even if it is possible to allocate the profit to the garment. Similarly, if someone buys a slave for a thousand dirhams, then sells him (before paying the price) to the same seller along with another slave for one thousand five hundred, the sale is not valid regarding the first slave, even if it is possible to correct it by allocating the thousand to him. Similarly, if someone combines his slave and another's slave and says 'I sell you one of them', it is not valid, even if it is possible to correct it by allocating it to his own slave. Similarly, if one sells a dirham and a garment for a dirham and a garment, and they separate without taking possession, the contract is invalid regarding the two dirhams, and the dirham is not turned to the garment, for the reason we mentioned.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning a sale of bundled currency where each party gives two items and receives two items, but the quantities per type differ. The Hanafi position, attributed here to Imam Muhammad (or the general Hanafi view), deems the sale valid by reinterpreting the consideration: each dirham given is considered to be in exchange for a dinar received, and vice versa. This avoids riba, as equal exchange of dirhams or dinars requires immediate hand-to-hand equality. Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that changing the intended consideration is a change in the parties' disposition, which is impermissible even if it corrects the transaction, because the contract is built on the total consideration not being specifically assigned. The passage provides analogies: a murabaha sale where profit cannot be arbitrarily assigned to one item; a resale of a slave before payment with an additional slave; and an ambiguous sale of one of two slaves. In all these, even if a correct interpretation is possible, the contract is invalid because the actual disposition is defective. The Urdu sharh explains that the valid Hanafi ruling is based on the principle of saving the contract from riba by applying the consideration to the opposite type, a form of istihsan. The dispute is practical: the Hanafi school allows the contract, while Zufar and Shafi'i prohibit it.

Key Terms
صرف (sarf): Exchange of gold and silver (currency) with immediate hand-to-hand delivery to avoid riba.
ربوا (riba): Usury or interest, specifically riba al-fadl: excess in exchange of same genus without immediate possession.
تغيير التصرف (taghyir al-tasarruf): Changing the disposition or legal effect of a contract from what was intended by the parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) By considering each dirham in exchange for a dirham of the same type.
B) By placing each type in exchange for the opposite type (dirhams for dinars).
C) By requiring immediate handover only.
D) By adding a condition of free consent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates mixed currency exchanges with unequal counts by reinterpreting the consideration, using istihsan to avoid riba.

Redirecting Genres to Avoid Riba: A Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Explaining a multi-part exchange dispute with opponent's examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on redirecting species in an exchange to avoid riba, and evaluate the Shafi'i/Zufar counter-argument based on change of disposal.

Core Idea
To avoid riba, each genus can be redirected to its opposite, even if it changes the parties' disposal.

This lesson explores a classical dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam Zufar/Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the permissibility of exchanging two dirhams and one dinar for one dirham and two dinars. The Hanafis allow redirecting each genus to its opposite to eliminate riba, while the opponents argue that such redirection impermissibly alters the parties' disposal. We will analyze the reasoning behind both positions through the text of Ashraf al-Hidayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
State the exact exchange: two dirhams + one dinar for one dirham + two dinars. Note that if each genus were exchanged for itself, riba would occur (two dirhams for one dirham – excess; one dinar for two dinars – excess).
2
State the Hanafi solution
The Hanafi school redirects each genus to its opposite: two dirhams against two dinars, one dinar against one dirham. This eliminates same-genus excess and makes the contract valid.
3
Identify the opposing view
Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i reject this because it changes the original disposal of the parties. They argue that when a total is exchanged for a total, the division must be by shuyu' (commonality) – every part of each countervalue against every part of the other – so specific assignment is not permissible.
Shuyu' means every part of each countervalue is opposed to every part of the other countervalue.
4
Work through the opponents' examples
Examine the three examples given: (1) Murabahah sale of a bracelet (10 dirhams weight) and a cloth (10 dirhams price) for 25 dirhams – profit cannot be redirected to cloth alone because it changes disposal; (2) Selling two slaves for 1500 dirhams before paying the 1000 dirhams for one of them – assigning 1000 to that slave would avoid the prohibition but changes the disposal; (3) Selling one of two slaves unspecified – redirecting to one's own slave would correct but changes disposal.
The third example is only mentioned but not fully elaborated in the passage.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis permit such exchanges (and similar redirective sales), while Shafi'is and Zufar prohibit them. This affects everyday currency and commodity trades where the parties wish to avoid riba by restructuring.

كتاب الصرف
اشرف البدایہ جلد-نم
الالف اليه وكذا اذا جمع بين عبده وعبد غيره وقال بعتك احدهما لا يجوزو ان امكن تصحيحه بصرفه الى عبده وكذا اذا باع درهما وثوبا بدرهم وثوب وافترقا من غير قبض فسد العقد في الدرهمين ولا يصرف الدرهم الى الثوب لما ذكرنا
ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے دو درہم اور ایک دینار کو ایک درہم اور دو دینار کے عوض بیچا تو یہ بیج جائز ہے اور ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک جنس کو اس کے خلاف کے عوض قرار دیا جائے گا۔ اور امام زفر اور امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ جائز نہیں ہے اور اسی اختلاف پر یہ ہے کہ جب ایک کر جو اور ایک گر گندم، دو گر گندم اور دو کر جو کے عوض بیچا۔ امام زفر اور امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ خلاف جنس کی طرف پھیرنے میں اس کے تصرف کو تغیر کر نالا زم آتا ہے کیونکہ اس نے مجموعہ کا مجموعہ کے ساتھ مقابلہ کیا ہے اور اس کا تقاضہ مشترکہ طور پر ہوارہ ہے نہ کہ متعین طور پر اور تصرف کو متغیر کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ اگر چہ اس میں تصرف کو صحیح کرنا ہے جیسے کسی نے ایک کنگن دس درہم کے عوض اور ایک کپڑا دس درہم کے عوض خریدا پھر ان دونوں کو مرابط فروخت کیا تو جائز نہیں ہے۔ اگر چہ نفع کو کپڑے کی طرف پھیر ناممکن ہے اور اسی طرح اگر ایک غلام ایک ہزار درہم کے عوض خریدا پھر اس کو شمن ادا کرنے سے پہلے بائع کے ہاتھ دوسرے غلام کے ساتھ پندرہ سو درہم کے عوض فروخت کر دیا تو ایک ہزار کے عوض خریدے ہوئے غلام میں بیج جائز نہیں ہے۔ اگر چہ ایک ہزار کو اس کی طرف پھیر کر عقد کو صحیح بنانا ممکن ہے۔ اور اسی طرح اگر اپنے غلام کو اور دوسرے کے غلام کو ملا کر کہا کہ میں نے تیرے ہاتھ ان دونوں میں سے ایک کو فروخت کر دیا تو جائز نہیں ہے۔ اگر چہ اس بیع کو اس کے غلام کی طرف پھیر کر مسیح بنا ناممکن ہے اور اسی طرح اگر ایک درہم اور ایک کپڑے کو ایک درہم اور ایک کپڑے کے عوض فروخت کیا اور بغیر قبضہ کے دونوں عاقد جدا ہو گئے تو دونوں درہموں میں عقد فاسد ہو جائے گا اور درہم کو کپڑے کی طرف نہیں پھیرا جائے گا۔ اس دلیل کی وجہ سے جو ہم نے ذکر کی ہے۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی نے دو درہم اور ایک دینار کو ایک درہم اور دو دینار کے عوض فروخت کیا تو ہمارے نزدیک یہ بیع جائز ہے اور دونوں میں سے ہر ایک جنس کو اسکے خلاف کے عوض قرار دیا جائے گا۔ یعنی دو درہم ، دو دینار کے مقابلہ میں شمار ہوں گے اور ایک درہم ایک دینار کے عوض شمار ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اگر خلاف جنس کی طرف نہ پھیرا گیا بلکہ ہر ایک کو اس کی جنس کے مقابل قرار دیا گیا یعنی دو در ہم ایک درہم کے مقابلہ میں اور ایک دینار دو دینار کے مقابلہ میں ہوا لازم آئے گا اور ر ہوا چونکہ حرام اور نا جائز ہے۔ اسلئے یہ عقد بھی نا جائز ہوگا۔ پس عاقدین کے عقد کو صحیح بنانے کیلئے ہر ایک جنس کو اس کے خلاف جنس کی طرف پھیرا گیا ہے اور امام زفر اور امام شافعی نے فرمایا ہے کہ یہ عقد جائز نہیں ہے۔ یہی اختلاف اس وقت ہے جبکہ ایک کر جو اور ایک گر گندم دو گر گندم اور دو کر جو کے عوض فروخت کئے گئے تو ہمارے نزدیک جائز ہے اور ایک کر جو کو دو گر گندم کے عوض اور ایک گر گندم کو دو کر جو کے عوض قرار دیا جائے گا۔ لیکن امام زفر اور امام شافعی کے نزدیک نا جائز ہے۔ امام زفر اور امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ عوفین میں سے ہر ایک کو اس کی جنس کے مقابل قرار دینے میں یعنی دو درہم کو ایک درہم کے مقابل اور ایک دینار کو دو دینار کے مقابل قرار دینے میں تو ربوا لازم آتا ہے اور ر بوانا جائز ہے اور خلاف جنس کی طرف پھیرنے میں یعنی دود، ہم کو دور بینار کے عوض اور ایک دینار کو ایک درہم کے عوض قرار دینے میں عاقدین کے تصرف کو متغیر کرنا لازم آتا ہے۔ کیونکہ عاقدین نے دو درہم اور ایک دینار کے مجموعہ کا ایک درہم اور دو دینار کے مجموعہ کے ساتھ مقابلہ کیا ہے اور تقابل کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ بنوارہ
